  <script>

    window.docCookies = {
      getItem: function (sKey) {
        if (!sKey) { return null; }
        return decodeURIComponent(document.cookie.replace(new RegExp("(?:(?:^|.*;)\\s*" + encodeURIComponent(sKey).replace(/[\-\.\+\*]/g, "\\$&") + "\\s*\\=\\s*([^;]*).*$)|^.*$"), "$1")) || null;
      },
      setItem: function (sKey, sValue, vEnd, sPath, sDomain, bSecure) {
        if (!sKey || /^(?:expires|max\-age|path|domain|secure)$/i.test(sKey)) { return false; }
        var sExpires = "";
        if (vEnd) {
          switch (vEnd.constructor) {
            case Number:
              sExpires = vEnd === Infinity ? "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT" : "; max-age=" + vEnd;
              break;
            case String:
              sExpires = "; expires=" + vEnd;
              break;
            case Date:
              sExpires = "; expires=" + vEnd.toUTCString();
              break;
          }
        }
        document.cookie = encodeURIComponent(sKey) + "=" + encodeURIComponent(sValue) + sExpires + (sDomain ? "; domain=" + sDomain : "") + (sPath ? "; path=" + sPath : "") + (bSecure ? "; secure" : "");
        return true;
      },
      removeItem: function (sKey, sPath, sDomain) {
        if (!this.hasItem(sKey)) { return false; }
        document.cookie = encodeURIComponent(sKey) + "=; expires=Thu, 01 Jan 1970 00:00:00 GMT" + (sDomain ? "; domain=" + sDomain : "") + (sPath ? "; path=" + sPath : "");
        return true;
      },
      hasItem: function (sKey) {
        if (!sKey) { return false; }
        return (new RegExp("(?:^|;\\s*)" + encodeURIComponent(sKey).replace(/[\-\.\+\*]/g, "\\$&") + "\\s*\\=")).test(document.cookie);
      },
      keys: function () {
        var aKeys = document.cookie.replace(/((?:^|\s*;)[^\=]+)(?=;|$)|^\s*|\s*(?:\=[^;]*)?(?:\1|$)/g, "").split(/\s*(?:\=[^;]*)?;\s*/);
        for (var nLen = aKeys.length, nIdx = 0; nIdx < nLen; nIdx++) { aKeys[nIdx] = decodeURIComponent(aKeys[nIdx]); }
        return aKeys;
      }
    };

    window.identify = function() {
        analytics.identify();
    }
  </script>

   <script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
    $(document).ready(function(){
      // Hide Header on on scroll down
      var didScroll;
      var lastScrollTop = 0;
      var delta = 5;
      var headerHeight = $('header').outerHeight();
  
      $(window).scroll(function(event){
        didScroll = true;
      });
  
      setInterval(function() {
        if (didScroll) {
          hasScrolled();
          didScroll = false;
        }
      }, 250);
  
      function hasScrolled() {
        var st = $(this).scrollTop();
    
        // Make sure they scroll more than delta
        if(Math.abs(lastScrollTop - st) <= delta)
          return;
    
        // If they scrolled down and are past the navbar, add class .nav-up.
        // This is necessary so you never see what is "behind" the navbar.
        if (st > lastScrollTop && st > headerHeight){
          // Scroll Down
          $('.navigation__header').removeClass('header-down').addClass('header-up');
        } else {
          // Scroll Up
          if(st + $(window).height() < $(document).height()) {
            $('.navigation__header').removeClass('header-up').addClass('header-down');
          }
        }
    
        lastScrollTop = st;
      }
    });

    // Collapsable Search Field
    $('.search-field__holder.collapsed').on('click', function(e) {
      $(this).removeClass('collapsed').addClass('expanded').find('.search-field__input').focus();
    });

    $(document).on('touchstart', function() {
      documentClick = true;
    });
    $(document).on('touchmove', function() {
      documentClick = false;
    });

    $(document).on('click touchend', function (event) {
      if (event.type == 'click') documentClick = true;
      if (documentClick){
        if(!$(event.target).closest('.navigation__header .search-field__holder').length) {
          if($('.navigation__header .search-field__holder').hasClass('expanded')) {
            $('.navigation__header .search-field__holder').addClass('collapsed').removeClass('expanded');
          }
        }
      }
    })
  </script>

     <p>JavaScript® (often shortened to JS) is a lightweight, interpreted, object-oriented language with first-class functions, most known as the scripting language for Web pages, but used in many non-browser environments as well such as node.js or Apache CouchDB. It is a prototype-based, multi-paradigm scripting language that is dynamic, and supports object-oriented, imperative, and functional programming styles.</p>

                $(function() {
                  var completed = $('#completed-icon-2562-true'),
                      uncompleted = $('#uncompleted-icon-2562-true'),
                      tile = $('#lesson-tile-2562'),
                      row = $('#lesson-row-2562');

                  $("#lesson-row-2562 td").on("click", function(e) {
                    if( $(this).hasClass('cell-add-to') ) return;
                    if (e.isDefaultPrevented() || e.metaKey || e.ctrlKey || e.which === 2) {
                      return;
                    }
                    document.location = "https://egghead.io/lessons/mocking-an-ajax-request-when-testing-epics";
                  });

                  completed.hide();
                  uncompleted.show();

                  completed.click(function(event) {
                    event.preventDefault();
                    event.stopImmediatePropagation();
                    completed.hide();
                    uncompleted.show();
                    row.removeClass('lesson-watched');
                    tile.removeClass('lesson-watched');

                      type: "POST",

                      data: {
                        lesson_view: {

                      },
                      dataType: "json"
                    })

                    completed.show();

                    tile.addClass('lesson-watched');

        <div class="row block-lessons"> 
         <div class="col-sm-4 item-holder "> 
          <div id="lesson-tile-2562" class="item "> 
           <a href="/lessons/mocking-an-ajax-request-when-testing-epics"> 
            <div class="media-holder"> 
             <div class="overlay"></div> 
             <img src="https://dcv19h61vib2d.cloudfront.net/thumbs/egghead-js-mocking-an-ajax-request-when-testing-epics/egghead-js-mocking-an-ajax-request-when-testing-epics.jpg" alt="js tutorial about Mocking an ajax request when testing epics" class="img-responsive"> 
            </div> </a> 
           <!-- video-holder --> 
           <div class="media-subscription-holder"> 
            <h4 class="title"> <span class="pseudo-checkbox-holder"> <i id="completed-icon-2562-false" class="pseudo-checkbox" style="display: none"></i> <i id="uncompleted-icon-2562-false" class="pseudo-checkbox" style="display: none"></i> </span> Mocking an ajax request when testing epics </h4> 
            <div class="info-line"> 
             <small class="duration"><i class="icon-time"></i> 8:02</small> 
             <small class="folder"><i class="icon-folder-open-alt"></i> js</small> 
            </div> 
            <!-- info-line --> 
            <small class="description"><p>Often in unit tests we are focussing on the logic involved in crafting a network request, &amp; how we respond to the result. The external service is unlikely to be under our control, so we need a way to ‘mock’ the Ajax request in a way that allows us to focus on the logic. In this lesson we’ll see how we can pass in dependencies into epics to make testing things Ajax requests easier.</p> </small> 
           </div> 
           <!-- video-subscription-holder --> 
          </div> 
          <!-- item --> 
         </div> 
         <script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
  $(function() {
    var completed = $('#completed-icon-2562-false'),
        uncompleted = $('#uncompleted-icon-2562-false'),
        tile = $('#lesson-tile-2562'),
        row = $('#lesson-row-2562');



    completed.hide();
    uncompleted.show();

    completed.click(function() {
      completed.hide();
      uncompleted.show();
      row.removeClass('lesson-watched');
      tile.removeClass('lesson-watched');
      $.ajax({
        type: "POST",
        url: '/watch/manual_uncomplete',
        data: {
          lesson_view: {
            lesson_id: 2562
          }
        },
        dataType: "json"
      })
    });

    uncompleted.click(function() {
      completed.show();
      uncompleted.hide();
      row.addClass('lesson-watched');
      tile.addClass('lesson-watched');

         <div class="col-sm-4 item-holder "> 
          <div id="lesson-tile-2231" class="item "> 
           <a href="/lessons/use-dynamic-data-in-a-glimmerjs-component"> 
            <div class="media-holder"> 
             <div class="overlay"></div> 
            </div> </a> 
           <!-- video-holder --> 
           <div class="media-subscription-holder"> 
            <h4 class="title"> <span class="pseudo-checkbox-holder"> <i id="completed-icon-2231-false" class="pseudo-checkbox" style="display: none"></i> <i id="uncompleted-icon-2231-false" class="pseudo-checkbox" style="display: none"></i> </span> Use dynamic data in a GlimmerJS component </h4> 
            <div class="info-line"> 
             <small class="duration"><i class="icon-time"></i> 2:08</small> 
             <small class="folder"><i class="icon-folder-open-alt"></i> js</small> 
             <small class="custom-label custom-label-red"><strong>PRO</strong></small> 
            </div> 
            <!-- info-line --> 
            <small class="description"><p>In this lesson we will create an <code>updateContent</code> method that sets the value of our <code>content</code> property to the current epoch timestamp. We need to call <code>toString()</code> on the <code>Date.now()</code> method in order to make TypeScript happy.</p> <pre class="highlight plaintext"><code>updateContent() {
  this.content = Date.now().toString()
}
</code></pre> <p>We create a constructor that takes the options parameter, call <code>super(options)</code> and call into our <code>this.updateContent()</code> method.</p> <pre class="highlight plaintext"><code>constructor(options) {
  super(options)
  this.updateContent()
}
</code></pre> <p>When we refresh our page we can see that the value of <code>content</code> is set to the epoch timestamp. </p> <p>To make the content really dynamic we use <code>setInterval()</code> to call into <code>updateContent</code> each second: <code>setInterval(() =&gt; this.updateContent(), 1000)</code>.</p> <p>When we save our file we see that our content does not get updated anymore and when we check the console we see that we get an error telling us that: The 'content' property on the my-component component was changed after it had been rendered. The error also informs us that we should Use the @tracked decorator to mark this as a tracked property.</p> <p>We add the @tracked decorator to our property, we import tracked from @glimmer/component and we refresh the page. We verify that <code>content</code> gets updated each second.</p> <p>We change our <code>updateContent</code> to add our the timestamp to the array. When we use <code>this.items.push(this.content)</code> to achieve this we see that the list does not get updated. This is because the <code>@tracked</code> decorator only tracks the re-assignment of properties.</p> <p>The way to get this working is by re-assigning our <code>items</code> array inside <code>updateContent</code>. We assign a new array, de-structure the current items array and add the new content.</p> <pre class="highlight plaintext"><code>this.items = [...this.items, this.content]
</code></pre> </small> 
           </div> 
           <!-- video-subscription-holder --> 
          </div> 
          <!-- item --> 
         </div>

           <!-- video-holder --> 
           <div class="media-subscription-holder"> 
            <h4 class="title"> <span class="pseudo-checkbox-holder"> <i id="completed-icon-2230-false" class="pseudo-checkbox" style="display: none"></i> <i id="uncompleted-icon-2230-false" class="pseudo-checkbox" style="display: none"></i> </span> Use template variables in a GlimmerJS component </h4> 
            <div class="info-line"> 
             <small class="duration"><i class="icon-time"></i> 1:22</small> 
             <small class="folder"><i class="icon-folder-open-alt"></i> js</small> 
             <small class="custom-label custom-label-red"><strong>PRO</strong></small> 
            </div> 
            <!-- info-line --> 
            <small class="description"><p>In this lesson we will add a variable called <code>content</code> to our component class, initialize it as a string and display the variable in our class using the double curly braces syntax, which is the handlebars expression for expressions, <code>{ {content }}</code>. </p> <p>After this we create a new class variable called <code>items</code>, initialize it as an array with strings. To display this list in our template we add a <code>&lt;ul&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;</code> and inside this unordered list we use the handlebars <code>each</code> helper to display the list items.</p> <p>And inside our each statement we will print a <code>&lt;li&gt;</code> tag and render <code>{{ item}}</code> inside it.</p> <pre class="highlight plaintext"><code>&lt;ul&gt;
  {{#each items key="@index" as |item| }}
    &lt;li&gt;{{item}}&lt;/li&gt; 
  {{/each}}
&lt;/ul&gt;
</code></pre> </small> 
           </div>

             <small class="duration"><i class="icon-time"></i> 8:00</small> 
             <small class="folder"><i class="icon-folder-open-alt"></i> js</small>

            <!-- info-line --> 
            <small class="description"><p>Sanctuary.js is a functional programming library for JavaScript. It's similar to Ramda, but more strict and includes extra features, like the error handling monads Maybe &amp; Either. It's also similar to Folktale, but primarily takes influence from Haskell &amp; PureScript instead of Scala. In this lesson, we use Sanctuary's Traverse and the Maybe monad to safely process lists of data while gaining insight into the relationship between Traverse &amp; Sequence.</p> </small>

         <script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">

        uncompleted = $('#uncompleted-icon-2459-false'),

      tile.removeClass('lesson-watched');

          <div id="lesson-tile-2202" class="item "> 
           <a href="/lessons/javascript-understand-electron-s-main-and-renderer-process-architecture"> 
            <div class="media-holder"> 
             <div class="overlay"></div> 
             <img src="https://dcv19h61vib2d.cloudfront.net/thumbs/javascript-understand-electron-s-main-and-renderer-process-architecture/javascript-understand-electron-s-main-and-renderer-process-architecture.jpg" alt="js tutorial about Understand Electron’s main and renderer process architecture" class="img-responsive"> 
            </div> </a> 
           <!-- video-holder --> 
           <div class="media-subscription-holder"> 
            <h4 class="title"> <span class="pseudo-checkbox-holder"> <i id="completed-icon-2202-false" class="pseudo-checkbox" style="display: none"></i> <i id="uncompleted-icon-2202-false" class="pseudo-checkbox" style="display: none"></i> </span> Understand Electron’s main and renderer process architecture </h4> 
            <div class="info-line"> 
             <small class="duration"><i class="icon-time"></i> 3:28</small> 
             <small class="folder"><i class="icon-folder-open-alt"></i> js</small> 
             <small class="custom-label custom-label-red"><strong>PRO</strong></small> 
            </div> 
            <!-- info-line --> 
            <small class="description"><p>Dealing with multiple processes in an application is new for many JavaScript developers. We’ll go over what exactly a process is and what the primary responsibilities of the main and renderer processes are. The main process is primarily responsible for creating native UI elements and managing the application lifecycle, while the renderer process runs the HTML based UI. Accordingly, different APIs are available in each process. </p> </small> 
           </div> 
           <!-- video-subscription-holder --> 
          </div> 
          <!-- item --> 
         </div>

             <div class="overlay"></div> 
             <img src="https://dcv19h61vib2d.cloudfront.net/thumbs/egghead-beating-js-performance-optimizing-collision-detection-in-wasm-via-a-linked-list-grid/egghead-beating-js-performance-optimizing-collision-detection-in-wasm-via-a-linked-list-grid.jpg" alt="js tutorial about Surpass JS Performance with Optimized Collision Detection in WASM using a Linked List Grid" class="img-responsive">

            <h4 class="title"> <span class="pseudo-checkbox-holder"> <i id="completed-icon-2247-false" class="pseudo-checkbox" style="display: none"></i> <i id="uncompleted-icon-2247-false" class="pseudo-checkbox" style="display: none"></i> </span> Surpass JS Performance with Optimized Collision Detection in WASM using a Linked List Grid </h4> 
            <div class="info-line"> 
             <small class="duration"><i class="icon-time"></i> 6:43</small> 
             <small class="folder"><i class="icon-folder-open-alt"></i> js</small> 
             <small class="custom-label custom-label-red"><strong>PRO</strong></small> 
            </div> 
            <!-- info-line --> 
            <small class="description"><p>To write a grid collision detection optimization in C requires thinking about the data structure of the grid more than in JS. In C we can represent this with a linked list and show how to convert the JS optimization code into this structure. When we compare the final performance, the WebAssembly code is now faster than the JS code - being able to write a low-level, more efficient data structure in memory is what gives WebAssembly its best advantage over JS. For those new to the principles, a good introduction to data structures and algorithms is the CLRS Introduction to Algorithms book.</p> <p>Demo repo: <a href="https://github.com/guybedford/wasm-demo">https://github.com/guybedford/wasm-demo</a></p> <p><em>Note the demo is currently only supported in Chrome Canary with the Experimental Web Platform flag (chrome://flags/#enable-experimental-web-platform-feature) enabled due to the use of ES modules and WebGL 2.0.</em></p> </small>

            <h4 class="title"> <span class="pseudo-checkbox-holder"> <i id="completed-icon-2235-false" class="pseudo-checkbox" style="display: none"></i> <i id="uncompleted-icon-2235-false" class="pseudo-checkbox" style="display: none"></i> </span> Refactor Business Logic to Composable Functions with Lodash </h4> 
            <div class="info-line"> 
             <small class="duration"><i class="icon-time"></i> 4:45</small> 
             <small class="folder"><i class="icon-folder-open-alt"></i> js</small> 
             <small class="custom-label custom-label-red"><strong>PRO</strong></small>

            <!-- info-line --> 
            <small class="description"><p>Imagine if looking at your business logic read as simple as a story from a children's book. That's exactly what we'll cover by learning how to compose small pieces of business logic and also set them up for further composition in the future.</p> <p>We're showing a list of fictional tweets in a trivial React component created with create-react-app, however the example is not React specific and can be applied to any framework/environment.</p> <p>Our example will refactor out business logic from a non-composable function to small composable pieces. To accomplish this we'll be using flow, filter and map from lodash/fp, which is included in the regular lodash npm package.</p> <p>This approach works well in any environment where you're not relying on object references to your original data, as our functional approach creates new collection objects instead of mutating them.</p> <p>Guide to lodash/fp: <a href="https://github.com/lodash/lodash/wiki/FP-Guide">https://github.com/lodash/lodash/wiki/FP-Guide</a></p> </small>
